<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Second voyage of HMS <i>Beagle</i></h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HMS_Beagle_by_Conrad_Martens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="821" data-file-width="1235" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/HMS_Beagle_by_Conrad_Martens.jpg/400px-HMS_Beagle_by_Conrad_Martens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/HMS_Beagle_by_Conrad_Martens.jpg/600px-HMS_Beagle_by_Conrad_Martens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/HMS_Beagle_by_Conrad_Martens.jpg/800px-HMS_Beagle_by_Conrad_Martens.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HMS_Beagle_by_Conrad_Martens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A watercolour by <a href="/wiki/HMS_Beagle" title="HMS Beagle">HMS <i>Beagle</i></a>'s <a href="/wiki/Technical_drawing" title="Technical drawing">draughtsman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Martens" title="Conrad Martens">Conrad Martens</a>. Painted during the survey of <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a>, it depicts native Fuegians hailing the <i>Beagle</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>second voyage of HMS <i>Beagle</i></b>, from 27 December 1831 to 2 October 1836, was the second survey expedition of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Beagle" title="HMS Beagle">HMS <i>Beagle</i></a>, under captain <a href="/wiki/Robert_FitzRoy" title="Robert FitzRoy">Robert FitzRoy</a> who had taken over command of the ship on its first voyage after the previous captain committed suicide. FitzRoy had already thought of the advantages of having an expert in <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> on board, and sought a gentleman <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">naturalist</a> to accompany them as a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/supernumerary" title="wikt:supernumerary">supernumerary</a>. The young graduate <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> had hoped to see the tropics before becoming a <a href="/wiki/Parson" title="Parson">parson</a>, and accepted the opportunity. He was greatly influenced by reading <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lyell" title="Charles Lyell">Charles Lyell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Geology" title="Principles of Geology">Principles of Geology</a></i> during the voyage. By the end of the expedition, Darwin had already made his name as a geologist and <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> collector, and the publication of his journal which became known as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voyage_of_the_Beagle" title="The Voyage of the Beagle">The Voyage of the Beagle</a></i> gave him wide renown as a writer.</p>
<p>The <i>Beagle</i> sailed across the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, and then carried out detailed <a href="/wiki/Hydrographic_survey" title="Hydrographic survey">hydrographic surveys</a> around the coasts of the southern part of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, returning via <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> after having circumnavigated the Earth. While the expedition was originally planned to last two years, it lasted almost five.</p>
<p>Darwin spent most of this time exploring on land: three years and three months on land, 18 months at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-intro-1">[1]</a></sup> Early in the voyage he decided that he could write a book about geology, and he showed a gift for theorising. At <a href="/wiki/Punta_Alta" title="Punta Alta">Punta Alta</a> he made a major find of gigantic fossils of extinct mammals, then known from only a very few specimens. He ably collected and made detailed observations of plants and animals, with results that shook his belief that species were fixed and provided the basis for ideas which came to him when back in England, and led to his theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aims_of_the_expedition">Aims of the expedition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Aims of the expedition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Museum_Marine_Chronometer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2496" data-file-width="1512" height="363" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/British_Museum_Marine_Chronometer.jpg/220px-British_Museum_Marine_Chronometer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/British_Museum_Marine_Chronometer.jpg/330px-British_Museum_Marine_Chronometer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/British_Museum_Marine_Chronometer.jpg/440px-British_Museum_Marine_Chronometer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Museum_Marine_Chronometer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_chronometer_from_HMS_Beagle" title="Ship's chronometer from HMS Beagle">Ship's chronometer from HMS <i>Beagle</i></a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The main purpose of the expedition was to conduct a <a href="/wiki/Hydrographic_survey" title="Hydrographic survey">hydrographic survey</a> of the coasts of the southern part of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> as a continuation and correction of the work of previous surveys, to produce <a href="/wiki/Nautical_chart" title="Nautical chart">nautical charts</a> showing navigational and sea depth information for the navy and for commerce.</p>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> memorandum set out the detailed instructions. The first requirement was to resolve disagreements in the earlier surveys about the <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, which was essential as the base point for <a href="/wiki/Meridian_arc" title="Meridian arc">meridian distances</a>. The accurate <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">marine chronometers</a> needed to <a href="/wiki/Longitude_by_chronometer" title="Longitude by chronometer">determine longitude</a> had only become affordable since 1800; the <i>Beagle</i> carried <a href="/wiki/List_of_chronometers_on_HMS_Beagle" title="List of chronometers on HMS Beagle">22 chronometers</a> to allow corrections. The ship was to stop at specified points for four-day rating of the chronometers and to check them by <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">astronomical observations</a>: it was essential to take observations at <a href="/wiki/Praia" title="Praia">Porto Praya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fernando_de_Noronha" title="Fernando de Noronha">Fernando de Noronha</a> to calibrate against the previous surveys of <a href="/wiki/William_Fitzwilliam_Owen" title="William Fitzwilliam Owen">Owen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Foster_(scientist)" title="Henry Foster (scientist)">Foster</a>. It was important to survey the extent of the <a href="/wiki/Abrolhos_Archipelago" title="Abrolhos Archipelago">Abrolhos Archipelago</a> reefs, shown incorrectly in <a href="/wiki/Albin_Roussin" title="Albin Roussin">Roussin's</a> survey, then proceed to Rio de Janeiro to decide the exact longitude of <a href="/wiki/Villegagnon_Island" title="Villegagnon Island">Villegagnon Island</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-admiralty_1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-admiralty_1-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The "real work of the survey" was then to commence south of the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_de_la_Plata" title="Río de la Plata">Río de la Plata</a>, with return trips to <a href="/wiki/Montevideo" title="Montevideo">Montevideo</a> for supplies; details were given of priorities, including surveying <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> and approaches to harbours on the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a>. The west coast was then to be surveyed as far north as time and resources permitted. The commander would then determine his own route west: season permitting, he could survey the <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands" title="Galápagos Islands">Galápagos Islands</a>. Then the <i>Beagle</i> was to proceed to <a href="/wiki/1769_Transit_of_Venus_observed_from_Tahiti" title="1769 Transit of Venus observed from Tahiti">Point Venus,Tahiti</a> and on to <a href="/wiki/Port_Jackson" title="Port Jackson">Port Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> which were known points to verify the chronometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-admiralty_2_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-admiralty_2-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>No time was to be wasted on elaborate drawings; charts and plans should have notes and simple views of the land as seen from the sea showing measured heights of hills. Continued records of tides and <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorological</a> conditions were also required. An additional suggestion was for a geological survey of a circular coral <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atoll</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> including its profile and of tidal flows, to investigate the <a href="/wiki/The_Structure_and_Distribution_of_Coral_Reefs" title="The Structure and Distribution of Coral Reefs">formation of such coral reefs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-admiralty_3_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-admiralty_3-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Context_and_preparations">Context and preparations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Context and preparations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The previous survey expedition to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> involved <a href="/wiki/HMS_Aid_(1809)" title="HMS Aid (1809)">HMS <i>Adventure</i></a> and HMS <i>Beagle</i> under the overall command of the Australian Commander <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Parker_King" title="Phillip Parker King">Phillip Parker King</a>. During the survey <i>Beagle's</i> captain, <a href="/wiki/Pringle_Stokes" title="Pringle Stokes">Pringle Stokes</a>, committed suicide and command of the ship was given to the young aristocrat <a href="/wiki/Robert_FitzRoy" title="Robert FitzRoy">Robert FitzRoy</a>, a nephew of <a href="/wiki/George_FitzRoy,_4th_Duke_of_Grafton" title="George FitzRoy, 4th Duke of Grafton">George FitzRoy, 4th Duke of Grafton</a>. When a ship's boat was taken by native <a href="/wiki/Fuegians" title="Fuegians">Fuegians</a>, FitzRoy took some of them hostage. After their return to <a href="/wiki/HMNB_Devonport" title="HMNB Devonport">Devonport dockyard</a> on 14 October 1830 Captain King retired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 26-year-old FitzRoy had hopes of commanding a second expedition to continue the South American survey, but when he heard that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lords_of_the_Admiralty" title="Lords of the Admiralty">Lords of the Admiralty</a> no longer supported this, he grew concerned about how to return the Fuegians who had been taught English with the idea that they could become missionaries. He made an agreement with the owner of a small merchant-vessel to take himself and five others back to South America, but a kind uncle heard of this and contacted the <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a>. Soon afterwards FitzRoy heard that he was to be appointed commander of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Chanticleer_(1808)" title="HMS Chanticleer (1808)">HMS <i>Chanticleer</i></a> to go to <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a>, but due to her poor condition <i>Beagle</i> was substituted. On 27 June 1831 FitzRoy was commissioned as commander of the voyage, and Lieutenants <a href="/wiki/John_Clements_Wickham" title="John Clements Wickham">John Clements Wickham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Sulivan" title="Bartholomew Sulivan">Bartholomew James Sulivan</a> were appointed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fitz13_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-fitz13-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Captain <a href="/wiki/Francis_Beaufort" title="Francis Beaufort">Francis Beaufort</a>, the Hydrographer of the Admiralty, was invited to decide on the use that could be made of the voyage to continue the survey, and he discussed with FitzRoy plans for a voyage of several years, including a continuation of the trip around the world to establish median distances. The <i>Beagle</i> was <a href="/wiki/Ship_commissioning" title="Ship commissioning">commissioned</a> on 4 July 1831 under the command of Captain Robert FitzRoy, who promptly spared no expense in having the <i>Beagle</i> extensively refitted. The <i>Beagle</i> was immediately taken into dock for extensive rebuilding and refitting. As she required a new deck, FitzRoy had the upper deck raised considerably, by 8 inches (200 mm) aft and 12 inches (300 mm) forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fitz17_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-fitz17-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cherokee_class_brig-sloop" title="Cherokee class brig-sloop"><i>Cherokee</i> class brig-sloops</a> had the reputation of being "coffin brigs", which handled badly and were prone to sinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PortCities_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-PortCities-8">[8]</a></sup> By helping the decks to drain more quickly with less water collecting in the <a href="/wiki/Gunwale" title="Gunwale">gunnels</a>, the raised deck gave the <i>Beagle</i> better handling and made her less liable to become top-heavy and capsize. Additional sheathing to the hull added about seven tons to her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burthen" title="Burthen">burthen</a> and perhaps fifteen to her displacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fitz17_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-fitz17-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ship was one of the first to test the <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning conductor</a> invented by <a href="/wiki/William_Snow_Harris" title="William Snow Harris">William Snow Harris</a>. FitzRoy obtained five examples of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sympiesometer" title="Sympiesometer">Sympiesometer</a></i>, a kind of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>-free <a href="/wiki/Barometer" title="Barometer">barometer</a> patented by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Adie" title="Alexander Adie">Alexander Adie</a> and favoured by FitzRoy as giving the accurate readings required by the Admiralty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fitz17_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-fitz17-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to its officers and crew, the <i>Beagle</i> carried several <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/supernumerary" title="wikt:supernumerary">supernumeraries</a>, passengers without an official position. FitzRoy employed a mathematical instrument maker to maintain his <a href="/wiki/List_of_chronometers_on_HMS_Beagle" title="List of chronometers on HMS Beagle">22 marine chronometers kept in his cabin</a>, as well as engaging the artist/draughtsman <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Earle" title="Augustus Earle">Augustus Earle</a> to go in a private capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fitz17_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-fitz17-7">[7]</a></sup> The three Fuegians taken on the previous voyage were going to be returned to <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> on the <i>Beagle</i> together with the missionary Richard Matthews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fitz13_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-fitz13-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naturalist_and_geologist">Naturalist and geologist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Naturalist and geologist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For <a href="/wiki/Francis_Beaufort" title="Francis Beaufort">Beaufort</a> and the leading <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge</a> "gentlemen of science" the opportunity for a <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">naturalist</a> to join the expedition fitted with their drive to revitalise British government policy on science. This elite disdained research done for money and felt that <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> was for <a href="/wiki/Gentleman" title="Gentleman">gentlemen</a>, not <a href="/wiki/Tradesman" title="Tradesman">tradesmen</a>. The officer class of the Army and Navy provided a way to ascend this hierarchy; it was commonly the <a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_doctor" title="Ship's doctor">Ship's Surgeon</a> who collected specimens on voyages, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_McCormick_(explorer)" title="Robert McCormick (explorer)">Robert McCormick</a> had secured the official position of surgeon on the <i>Beagle</i> after taking part in earlier expeditions and studying natural history. A sizeable collection had considerable social value, attracting wide public interest, and McCormick aspired to fame as an exploring naturalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Collections made by the Ship's Surgeon and other officers were government property, though the Admiralty was not consistent on this,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_131_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_131-11">[11]</a></sup> and went to important London establishments, usually the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The Admiralty instructions for the first voyage had required officers "to use their best diligence in increasing the Collections in each ship: the whole of which must be understood to belong to the Public", but on the second voyage this requirement was omitted, and the officers were free to keep all the specimens for themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_131_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_131-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>FitzRoy's narrative recalls that, when investigating islands on the first voyage, he had regretted that no-one on board had expertise on <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> to make use of the opportunity of "ascertaining the nature of the rocks and earths" of the areas surveyed, and resolved that if on a similar expedition, he would "endeavour to carry out a person qualified to examine the land; while the officers, and myself, would attend to <a href="/wiki/Hydrography" title="Hydrography">hydrography</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-king-13">[13]</a></sup> This clearly indicated a need for a naturalist qualified to examine geology, who would spend considerable periods onshore away from the ship. McCormick lacked expertise in geology, and had to attend to his duties on the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Wyhe20133_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Wyhe20133-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>FitzRoy knew that commanding a ship could involve stress and loneliness; he was fully aware of the suicide of <a href="/wiki/Pringle_Stokes" title="Pringle Stokes">Captain Stokes</a>, and his own uncle <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscount_Castlereagh" title="Viscount Castlereagh">Viscount Castlereagh</a> had committed suicide under stress of overwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> For the first time he would be fully in charge with no commanding officer or second captain to consult, and it has been suggested that he felt the need for a gentleman companion who shared his scientific interests and could dine with him as an equal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> There is no direct evidence to support this. Henslow described the position "more as a companion than a mere collector", but this was an assurance that FitzRoy would treat his guest as a gentleman naturalist. Several other ships at this period carried unpaid civilians as naturalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Wyhe20135.E2.80.937_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Wyhe20135.E2.80.937-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early in August, FitzRoy discussed this position with Beaufort, who had a scientific network of friends at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro4_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-intro4-18">[18]</a></sup> At Beaufort's request, mathematics lecturer <a href="/wiki/George_Peacock" title="George Peacock">George Peacock</a> wrote from London to Professor <a href="/wiki/John_Stevens_Henslow" title="John Stevens Henslow">John Stevens Henslow</a> about this "rare opportunity for a naturalist", saying that an "offer has been made to me to recommend a proper person to go out as a naturalist with this expedition", and suggesting the Reverend <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Jenyns" title="Leonard Jenyns">Leonard Jenyns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_104_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_104-19">[19]</a></sup> Though Jenyns nearly accepted, and even packed his clothes, he had concerns about his obligations as vicar of <a href="/wiki/Swaffham_Bulbeck" title="Swaffham Bulbeck">Swaffham Bulbeck</a> and his health, so declined. Henslow briefly thought of going, but his wife "looked so miserable" that he quickly dropped the idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_115_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_115-20">[20]</a></sup> Both recommended the 22-year-old <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> who had just completed the ordinary <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> degree which was a prerequisite for his intended career as a <a href="/wiki/Parson" title="Parson">parson</a>, and was on a <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> field trip with <a href="/wiki/Adam_Sedgwick" title="Adam Sedgwick">Adam Sedgwick</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro4_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-intro4-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Offer_of_place_to_Darwin">Offer of place to Darwin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Offer of place to Darwin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Darwin fitted well the expectations of a gentleman natural philosopher, and was well trained as a naturalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Wyhe20136_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Wyhe20136-21">[21]</a></sup> When he had studied geology in his second year at Edinburgh he had found it dull, but from Easter to August 1831 he learned a great deal with <a href="/wiki/Adam_Sedgwick" title="Adam Sedgwick">Adam Sedgwick</a> and developed a strong interest during their geological field trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fieldnotesGalapagos_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-fieldnotesGalapagos-22">[22]</a></sup> On 24 August <a href="/wiki/John_Stevens_Henslow" title="John Stevens Henslow">Henslow</a> wrote to Darwin:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>...that I consider you to be the best qualified person I know of who is likely to undertake such a situation— I state this not on the supposition of yr. being a finished Naturalist, but as amply qualified for collecting, observing, &amp; noting any thing worthy to be noted in Natural History. Peacock has the appointment at his disposal &amp; if he can not find a man willing to take the office, the opportunity will probably be lost— Capt. F. wants a man (I understand) more as a companion than a mere collector &amp; would not take any one however good a Naturalist who was not recommended to him likewise as a <i>gentleman</i>. ... there never was a finer chance for a man of zeal &amp; spirit... Don't put on any modest doubts or fears about your disqualifications for I assure you I think you are the very man they are in search of.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter_105_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter_105-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The letter went first to <a href="/wiki/George_Peacock" title="George Peacock">George Peacock</a>, who quickly forwarded it to Darwin with further details, confirming that the "ship sails about the end of September". Peacock had discussed the offer with <a href="/wiki/Francis_Beaufort" title="Francis Beaufort">Beaufort</a>, "he entirely approves of it &amp; you may consider the situation as at your absolute disposal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter_106_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter_106-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin%27s_education#Voyage_on_the_Beagle" title="Charles Darwin's education">When Darwin returned home</a> late on 29 August and opened the letters, his father objected strongly to the voyage so the next day he wrote declining the offer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_107_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_107-25">[25]</a></sup> and left to go shooting at the estate of his uncle <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Wedgwood_II" title="Josiah Wedgwood II">Josiah Wedgwood II</a>. With Wedgwood's help, Darwin's father was persuaded to relent and fund his son's expedition, and on Thursday 1 September Darwin wrote accepting Peacock's offer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_112_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_112-26">[26]</a></sup> That day, Beaufort wrote to tell FitzRoy that his friend Peacock had "succeeded in getting a '<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/savant" title="wikt:savant">Savant</a>' for you—A Mr Darwin grandson of the well known philosopher and poet—full of zeal and enterprize and having contemplated a voyage on his own account to S. America".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_113_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_113-27">[27]</a></sup> On Friday Darwin left for Cambridge, where he spent Saturday with Henslow getting advice on preparations, and references to experts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_115_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_115-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander Charles Wood (an undergraduate whose tutor was Peacock) wrote from Cambridge to his cousin FitzRoy to recommend Darwin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Around midday on Sunday 4 September Wood received FitzRoy's response, "straightforward and gentlemanlike" but strongly against Darwin joining the expedition; both Darwin and Henslow then "gave up the scheme". Darwin went to London anyway, and next morning met FitzRoy who explained that he had promised the place to his friend Mr. Chester, (possibly the novelist Harry Chester) but Chester had turned it down in a letter received not five minutes before Darwin arrived. FitzRoy emphasised the difficulties including cramped conditions and plain food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_117_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_117-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_118_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_118-30">[30]</a></sup> Darwin would be on the Admiralty's books to get provisions (worth £40 a year) and, like the ship's officers and captain, would pay £30 a year towards the <a href="/wiki/Mess" title="Mess">mess</a> bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_132_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_132-31">[31]</a></sup> Including outfitting, the cost to him was unlikely to reach £500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_117_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_117-29">[29]</a></sup> The ship would sail on 10 October, and would probably be away for three years. They talked and dined together, and soon found each other agreeable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_118_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_118-30">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tory</a> FitzRoy had been cautious at the prospect of companionship with this unknown young gentleman of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" title="British Whig Party">Whig</a> background, and later admitted that his letter to Wood was "to throw cold water on the scheme" in "a sudden horror of the chances of having somebody he should not like on board". He half-seriously told Darwin later that, as "an ardent disciple of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Kaspar_Lavater" title="Johann Kaspar Lavater">Lavater</a>", he had nearly rejected Darwin on the <a href="/wiki/Phrenology" title="Phrenology">phrenological</a> basis that the shape (or <a href="/wiki/Physiognomy" title="Physiognomy">physiognomy</a>) of Darwin's nose indicated a lack of determination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_122_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_122-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Darwin.27s_preparations">Darwin's preparations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Darwin's preparations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>While he continued to get acquainted with Fitzroy, going shopping together, Darwin rushed around to arrange his supplies and equipment, getting advice from experts on specimen preservation such as <a href="/wiki/William_Yarrell" title="William Yarrell">William Yarrell</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Zoological_Society_of_London" title="Zoological Society of London">Zoological Society of London</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Brown_(botanist)" title="Robert Brown (botanist)">Robert Brown</a> at the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>, Captain <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Parker_King" title="Phillip Parker King">Phillip Parker King</a> who led the first expedition, and invertebrate anatomist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Edmond_Grant" title="Robert Edmond Grant">Robert Edmond Grant</a> who had tutored Darwin at Edinburgh. Yarrell gave invaluable advice, and bargained with shopkeepers so Darwin paid £50 for two pistols and a rifle, while FitzRoy had spent £400 on firearms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_122_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_122-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_123_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_123-34">[34]</a></sup> On Sunday 11 September, FitzRoy and Darwin took the <a href="/wiki/Packet_trade" title="Packet trade">steam packet</a> for Portsmouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJ_7.E2.80.938_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-DJ_7.E2.80.938-35">[35]</a></sup> Darwin was not seasick, and had a pleasant "sail of three days". For the first time he saw the "very small" cramped ship, met the officers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_126_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_126-36">[36]</a></sup> and was glad to get a large cabin, shared with the assistant surveyor <a href="/wiki/John_Lort_Stokes" title="John Lort Stokes">John Lort Stokes</a>. On Friday Darwin rushed back to London, "250 miles in 24 hours",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_127_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_127-37">[37]</a></sup> and on via Cambridge to arrive in Shrewsbury on 22 September for a last quick visit to family and friends, leaving for London on 2 October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJ_7.E2.80.938_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-DJ_7.E2.80.938-35">[35]</a></sup> Delays to the <i>Beagle</i> gave Darwin an extra week to consult experts and complete packing his baggage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlDarwin_Correspondence_Project_.C2.BB_letter:_142_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlDarwin_Correspondence_Project_.C2.BB_letter:_142-38">[38]</a></sup> After sending his heavy goods down by steam packet, he took the coach along with <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Earle" title="Augustus Earle">Augustus Earle</a>, and arrived at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devonport,_Devon" title="Devonport, Devon">Devonport</a> on 24 October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlDarwin_Correspondence_Project_.C2.BB_letter:_139_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlDarwin_Correspondence_Project_.C2.BB_letter:_139-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The geologist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lyell" title="Charles Lyell">Charles Lyell</a> asked FitzRoy to record observations on geological features such as erratic boulders. Before they left England, FitzRoy gave Darwin a copy of the first volume of Lyell's <i>Principles of Geology</i> which explained features as the outcome of a gradual process taking place over extremely long periods of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In his autobiography Darwin recalled Henslow giving advice at this time to obtain and study the book, "but on no account to accept the views therein advocated".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k01p27_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-k01p27-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Darwin's position as a naturalist on board was as a self-funded guest with no official appointment, and he could leave the voyage at any suitable stage. At the outset <a href="/wiki/George_Peacock" title="George Peacock">George Peacock</a> had advised that "The Admiralty are not disposed to give a salary, though they will furnish you with an official appointment &amp; every accomodation [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>]: if a salary should be required however I am inclined to think that it would be granted". Far from wanting this,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter_106_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-letter_106-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_199_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_199-42">[42]</a></sup> Darwin's concern was to maintain control over his collection. He was even reluctant to be on the Admiralty's books for <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/victuals" title="wikt:victuals">victuals</a> until he got assurances from FitzRoy and Beaufort that this would not affect his rights to assign his specimens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_132_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_132-31">[31]</a></sup> Darwin did not want his collection to go to the British Museum as he had heard that specimens from the first <i>Beagle</i> voyage were still waiting to be described. Beaufort assured him that he "should have no difficulty" as long as he "presented them to some public body" such as the <a href="/wiki/Zoological_Society_of_London" title="Zoological Society of London">Zoological</a> or <a href="/wiki/Geological_Society_of_London" title="Geological Society of London">Geological</a> societies. Darwin himself thought his new finds should go to the "largest &amp; most central collection", not the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Philosophical_Society" title="Cambridge Philosophical Society">Cambridge Philosophical Society</a> museum that <a href="/wiki/John_Stevens_Henslow" title="John Stevens Henslow">Henslow</a> was setting up at Cambridge,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter:_123_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-letter:_123-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> but after getting Henslow's willing agreement to take delivery of the consignments of specimens, Darwin replied that he hoped to give some to the Cambridge museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter_144_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter_144-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Darwin.27s_work_on_the_expedition">Darwin's work on the expedition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Darwin's work on the expedition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The captain had to record his survey in painstaking paperwork, and Darwin too kept a daily log as well as detailed notebooks of his finds and speculations, and a diary which became his journal. Darwin's notebooks show a complete professionalism that he had probably learnt at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> when making natural history notes while exploring the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> with his brother Erasmus in 1826 and studying marine invertebrates with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Edmund_Grant" title="Robert Edmund Grant">Robert Edmund Grant</a> for a few months in 1827.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kix_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-kix-45">[45]</a></sup> Darwin had also collected <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a> at Cambridge, but he was a novice in all other areas of natural history. During the voyage Darwin investigated small invertebrates, while collecting specimens of other creatures for experts to examine and describe once the <i>Beagle</i> had returned to England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fieldnotesGalapagos_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-fieldnotesGalapagos-22">[22]</a></sup> More than half of his carefully organised zoology notes deal with marine invertebrates, and the notes record closely reasoned interpretations of what he found about their complex internal anatomy while dissecting specimens under his microscope, and of little experiments on their response to stimulation. His onshore observations included intense, analytical comments on possible reasons for the behaviour, distribution, and relation to their environment of the creatures he saw. He made good use of the ship's excellent library of books on natural history, but continually questioned their correctness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kx_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-kx-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Geology was Darwin's "principal pursuit" on the expedition and his notes on that subject were almost four times larger than his zoology notes, although he kept extensive records on both. During the voyage, he wrote to his sister that "there is nothing like geology; the pleasure of the first days partridge shooting or first days hunting cannot be compared to finding a fine group of fossil bones, which tell their story of former times with almost a living tongue". To him, investigating geology brought reasoning into play and gave him opportunities for theorising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kix_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-kix-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyage_of_the_Beagle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1399" data-file-width="2712" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Voyage_of_the_Beagle.jpg/500px-Voyage_of_the_Beagle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Voyage_of_the_Beagle.jpg/750px-Voyage_of_the_Beagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Voyage_of_the_Beagle.jpg/1000px-Voyage_of_the_Beagle.jpg 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyage_of_the_Beagle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The voyage of the <i>Beagle</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Voyage">Voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Charles Darwin had been told that the <i>Beagle</i> was expected to sail about the end of September 1831,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-letter_106_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-letter_106-24">[24]</a></sup> but fitting out took longer. The <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> Instructions were received on 14 November, and on 23 November she was moved to anchorage, ready to depart. Repeated Westerly gales caused delays, and forced them to turn back after departing on 10 and 21 December. Drunkenness at Christmas lost another day. Finally, on the morning of 27 December, the <i>Beagle</i> left its anchorage in the Barn Pool, under <a href="/wiki/Mount_Edgcumbe_Country_Park" title="Mount Edgcumbe Country Park">Mount Edgecumbe</a> on the west side of <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Sound" title="Plymouth Sound">Plymouth Sound</a> and set out on its surveying expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_islands">Atlantic islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Atlantic islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Beagle</i> touched at <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> for a confirmed position without stopping, Then on 6 January it reached <a href="/wiki/Tenerife" title="Tenerife">Tenerife</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, but was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarantined" title="Quarantined">quarantined</a> there because of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> in England. Although tantalisingly near to the town of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Cruz_de_Tenerife" title="Santa Cruz de Tenerife">Santa Cruz</a>, to Darwin's intense disappointment, they were denied landing. With improving weather conditions, they sailed on. On 10 January Darwin tried out a <a href="/wiki/Plankton_net" title="Plankton net">plankton net</a> he had devised to be towed behind the ship – only the second recorded use of such a net (after use by <a href="/wiki/John_Vaughan_Thompson" title="John Vaughan Thompson">John Vaughan Thompson</a> in 1816). Next day, he noted the great number of animals collected far from land and wrote "Many of these creatures so low in the scale of nature are most exquisite in their forms &amp; rich colours. — It creates a feeling of wonder that so much beauty should be apparently created for such little purpose."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Six days later they made their first landing at <a href="/wiki/Praia" title="Praia">Porto Praya</a> on the volcanic island of <a href="/wiki/Santiago,_Cape_Verde" title="Santiago, Cape Verde">St. Jago</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a> Islands. It is here that Darwin's description in his published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voyage_of_the_Beagle" title="The Voyage of the Beagle">Journal</a></i> begins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> His initial impression was of a desolate and sterile volcanic island, but after visiting the town he came to a deep valley where he "first saw the glory of tropical vegetation" and had "a glorious day", finding overwhelming novelty in the sights and sounds. FitzRoy set up tents and an observatory on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_Island,_Cape_Verde" title="Santa Maria Island, Cape Verde">Quail Island</a> to determine the exact position of the islands, while Darwin collected numerous sea animals, delighting in vivid tropical corals in tidal pools, and investigating the geology of Quail Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though <a href="/wiki/Charles_Daubeny" title="Charles Daubeny">Daubeny</a>'s book in the <i>Beagle'</i>s library described the volcanic geology of the Canary Islands, it said that the structure of the Cape Verde Islands was "too imperfectly known". Darwin saw Quail Island as his key to understanding the structure of St. Jago, and made careful studies of its <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">stratigraphy</a> in the way he had learnt from <a href="/wiki/Adam_Sedgwick" title="Adam Sedgwick">Adam Sedgwick</a>. He collected specimens and described a white layer of hard white rock formed from crushed coral and seashells lying between layers of black volcanic rocks, and noted a similar white layer running horizontally in the black cliffs of St. Jago at 40 feet (12 m) above sea level. The seashells were, as far as he could tell, "the same as those of present day". He speculated that in geologically recent times a lava flow had covered this shell sand on the sea bed, and then the strata had slowly risen to their present level. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lyell" title="Charles Lyell">Charles Lyell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Geology" title="Principles of Geology">Principles of Geology</a></i> presented a thesis of gradual rising and falling of the Earth's crust illustrated by the changing levels of the <a href="/wiki/Macellum_of_Pozzuoli" title="Macellum of Pozzuoli">Temple of Serapis</a>. Darwin implicitly supported Lyell by remarking that "Dr. Daubeny when mentioning the present state of the temple of Serapis. doubts the possibility of a surface of country being raised without cracking buildings on it. – I feel sure at St Jago in some places a town might have been raised without injuring a house."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In a letter to Henslow he wrote that "The geology was preeminently interesting &amp; I believe quite new: there are some facts on a large scale of upraised coast ... that would interest Mr. Lyell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter_171_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter_171-52">[52]</a></sup> While still on the island, Darwin was inspired to think of writing a book on geology,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and later wrote of "seeing a thing never seen by Lyell, one yet saw it partially through his eyes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Customarily the ship's surgeon took the position of naturalist, and the <i>Beagle'</i>s surgeon <a href="/wiki/Robert_McCormick_(explorer)" title="Robert McCormick (explorer)">Robert McCormick</a> sought fame and fortune as an explorer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-f196_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-f196-55">[55]</a></sup> When they first met at the start of the voyage, Darwin had commented that "My friend [McCormick] is an ass, but we jog on very amicably".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter_144_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Letter_144-44">[44]</a></sup> They walked into the countryside of St. Jago together,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and Darwin, influenced by Lyell, found the surgeon's approach old-fashioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter_171_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Letter_171-52">[52]</a></sup> They found a remarkable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baobab" title="Baobab">baobab</a> tree, which FitzRoy measured and sketched. Darwin went on subsequent "riding expeditions" with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Bynoe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Benjamin Bynoe (page does not exist)">Benjamin Bynoe</a> and Rowlett to visit <a href="/wiki/Cidade_Velha" title="Cidade Velha">Ribeira Grande</a> and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Domingos,_Cape_Verde" title="São Domingos, Cape Verde">St Domingo</a>. FitzRoy extended their stay to 23 days, to complete his measurements of <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_anomaly" title="Magnetic anomaly">magnetism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Darwin subsequently wrote to Henslow that his collecting included "several specimens of an Octopus, which possessed a most marvellous power of changing its colours; equalling any chamaelion, &amp; evidently accommodating the changes to the colour of the ground which it passed over.—yellowish green, dark brown &amp; red were the prevailing colours: this fact appears to be new, as far as I can find out."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter_171_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Letter_171-52">[52]</a></sup> Henslow replied that "The fact is not new, but any fresh observations will be highly important."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter_196_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter_196-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>McCormick increasingly resented the favours FitzRoy gave to assist Darwin with collecting. On 16 February, FitzRoy landed a small party including himself and Darwin on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Peter_and_Paul_Rocks" title="Saint Peter and Paul Rocks">St. Paul's rocks</a>, finding the seabirds so tame that they could be killed easily, while an exasperated McCormick was left circling the islets in a second small boat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> That evening novices were greeted by a pseudo-<a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Neptune</a>, and in the morning they crossed the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> with the traditional <a href="/wiki/Line-crossing_ceremony" title="Line-crossing ceremony">line-crossing ceremony</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Darwin had a special position as guest and social equal of the captain, so junior officers called him "sir" until the captain dubbed Darwin <i>Philos</i> for "ship's philosopher", and this became his suitably respectful nickname.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surveying_South_America">Surveying South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Surveying South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In South America, <i>Beagle</i> carried out its survey work going to and fro to along the coasts to allow careful measurement and rechecking. Darwin made long journeys inland with travelling companions from the locality. He spent much of the time away from the ship, returning by prearrangement when the <i>Beagle</i> returned to ports where mail could be received and Darwin's notes, journals, and collections sent back to England. He had ensured that his collections were his own and they were shipped back to <a href="/wiki/John_Stevens_Henslow" title="John Stevens Henslow">Henslow</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> to await his return. Several others on board including FitzRoy and other officers were able amateur naturalists, and they gave Darwin generous assistance as well as making collections for the Crown, which the <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> placed in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_paradise_and_slavery">Tropical paradise and slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tropical paradise and slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Due to heavy surf they only stayed at <a href="/wiki/Fernando_de_Noronha" title="Fernando de Noronha">Fernando de Noronha</a> for a day to make the required observations, and Fitzroy decided to make for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bahia,_Brazil" title="Bahia, Brazil">Bahia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, to rate the chronometers and take on water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-admiralty_1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-admiralty_1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> On 28 February they reached the continent, arriving at the magnificent sight of the town now known as <a href="/wiki/Salvador,_Bahia" title="Salvador, Bahia">Salvador</a>, with large ships at harbour scattered across the bay. On the next day, Darwin was in "transports of pleasure" walking by himself in the <a href="/wiki/Bahia_coastal_forests" title="Bahia coastal forests">tropical forest</a>, and in "long naturalizing walks" with others continued to "add raptures to the former raptures".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> He found the sights of slavery offensive and when FitzRoy defended the practice by describing a visit to a slaveowner whose slaves replied "no" on being asked by their master if they wished to be freed, Darwin suggested that answers in such circumstances were worthless. Enraged that his word had been questioned, FitzRoy lost his temper and banned Darwin from his company. The officers had nicknamed such outbursts "hot coffee," and within hours FitzRoy apologised and asked Darwin to remain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Later, FitzRoy had to remain silent when Captain Paget visited them and recounted "facts about slavery so revolting" that refuted his claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Surveying of sandbanks around the harbour was completed on 18 March, and the ship made its way down the coast to survey the extent and depths of the <a href="/wiki/Abrolhos_Archipelago" title="Abrolhos Archipelago">Abrolhos reefs</a>, completing and correcting <a href="/wiki/Albin_Roussin" title="Albin Roussin">Roussin's</a> survey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keynes_48_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-keynes_48-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 4 April they entered the harbour of <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, to make observations of <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> from <a href="/wiki/Villegagnon_Island" title="Villegagnon Island">Villegagnon Island</a>. Darwin took in the sights of the city then made an expedition into the interior, returning to the ship on 24 April. By then <a href="/wiki/Robert_McCormick_(explorer)" title="Robert McCormick (explorer)">Robert McCormick</a> had left the ship, with permission from the admiral in command, and returned to England. Assistant Surgeon <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Bynoe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Benjamin Bynoe (page does not exist)">Benjamin Bynoe</a> was made acting surgeon in his place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keynes_48_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-keynes_48-67">[67]</a></sup> McCormick felt "very much disappointed in my expectations of carrying out my natural history pursuits, every obstacle having been placed in the way of my getting on shore and making collections" while the gentleman Darwin received all the invitations from dignitaries onshore and was given facilities to pack his collections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><br/>
On 26 April Darwin moved into a house he had rented at <a href="/wiki/Botafogo" title="Botafogo">Botafogo</a>, and stayed there with three others when the <i>Beagle</i> left on 10 May to recheck observations at Bahia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keynes_48_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-keynes_48-67">[67]</a></sup> FitzRoy had found a discrepancy of 4 miles (6.4 km) in the meridian distance of longitude between his measurements and those of <a href="/wiki/Albin_Roussin" title="Albin Roussin">Albin Roussin</a>, and decided to go back. A seaman, a <a href="/wiki/Cabin_boy" title="Cabin boy">ship's boy</a> and a young <a href="/wiki/Midshipman" title="Midshipman">midshipman</a> had caught a fever after visiting the <a href="/wiki/Macacu_River" title="Macacu River">Macacu River</a>, and died. When the ship returned to Rio on 3 June, FitzRoy confirmed that his measurements of Bahia and of the Abrolhos reefs were correct, and sent these corrections to Roussin. They sailed from Rio on 5 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_finds">Fossil finds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fossil finds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After storms, the <i>Beagle</i> reached <a href="/wiki/Montevideo" title="Montevideo">Montevideo</a> on 26 July 1832, and took observations for the chronometers. An attempt to call at <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a> for information was thwarted by officials, then FitzRoy agreed a request for ship's crew (and Darwin) to briefly occupy a Montevideo fort to dispel a <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a>. On 22 August, after taking soundings in <a href="/wiki/Samboromb%C3%B3n_Bay" title="Samborombón Bay">Samborombón Bay</a>, the <i>Beagle</i> began survey work down the coast from <a href="/wiki/Cape_San_Antonio,_Argentina" title="Cape San Antonio, Argentina">Cape San Antonio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Earle_(presumed)_-_Quarter_Deck_of_a_Man_of_War_on_Diskivery_(sic)_or_interesting_Scenes_on_an_Interesting_Voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1213" data-file-width="2000" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Augustus_Earle_%28presumed%29_-_Quarter_Deck_of_a_Man_of_War_on_Diskivery_%28sic%29_or_interesting_Scenes_on_an_Interesting_Voyage.jpg/220px-Augustus_Earle_%28presumed%29_-_Quarter_Deck_of_a_Man_of_War_on_Diskivery_%28sic%29_or_interesting_Scenes_on_an_Interesting_Voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Augustus_Earle_%28presumed%29_-_Quarter_Deck_of_a_Man_of_War_on_Diskivery_%28sic%29_or_interesting_Scenes_on_an_Interesting_Voyage.jpg/330px-Augustus_Earle_%28presumed%29_-_Quarter_Deck_of_a_Man_of_War_on_Diskivery_%28sic%29_or_interesting_Scenes_on_an_Interesting_Voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Augustus_Earle_%28presumed%29_-_Quarter_Deck_of_a_Man_of_War_on_Diskivery_%28sic%29_or_interesting_Scenes_on_an_Interesting_Voyage.jpg/440px-Augustus_Earle_%28presumed%29_-_Quarter_Deck_of_a_Man_of_War_on_Diskivery_%28sic%29_or_interesting_Scenes_on_an_Interesting_Voyage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Earle_(presumed)_-_Quarter_Deck_of_a_Man_of_War_on_Diskivery_(sic)_or_interesting_Scenes_on_an_Interesting_Voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A caricature of the scene on the quarter deck while anchored at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bahia_Blanca" title="Bahia Blanca">Bahia Blanca</a>, painted around 24 September 1832. Darwin is the central figure in a top hat, Fitzroy the second figure to his left. The watercolour is attributed to the shipboard artist <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Earle" title="Augustus Earle">Augustus Earle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armitstead_2015_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armitstead_2015-71">[71]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%ADa_Blanca" title="Bahía Blanca">Bahía Blanca</a>, in the southern part of present <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires_Province" title="Buenos Aires Province">Buenos Aires Province</a>, Darwin rode inland into <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> with <a href="/wiki/Gaucho" title="Gaucho">gauchos</a>: he saw them use <a href="/wiki/Bolas" title="Bolas">bolas</a> to bring down "ostriches" (<a href="/wiki/Rhea_(bird)" title="Rhea (bird)">rheas</a>), and ate roast <a href="/wiki/Armadillo" title="Armadillo">armadillo</a>. With FitzRoy, he went for "a very pleasant cruize about the bay" on 22 September, and about ten miles (16 km) from the ship they stopped for a while at <a href="/wiki/Punta_Alta" title="Punta Alta">Punta Alta</a>. In low cliffs near the point Darwin found <a href="/wiki/Conglomerate_(geology)" title="Conglomerate (geology)">conglomerate rocks</a> containing numerous shells and fossilised teeth and bones of gigantic extinct <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> in strata near an earth layer with shells and <a href="/wiki/Armadillo" title="Armadillo">armadillo</a> fossils, suggesting to him quiet tidal deposits rather than a catastrophe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cpw_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-cpw-73">[73]</a></sup> With assistance (possibly including the young sailor <a href="/wiki/Syms_Covington" title="Syms Covington">Syms Covington</a> acting as his servant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JSC3_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-JSC3-74">[74]</a></sup>) Darwin collected numerous fossils over several days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D46PA_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-D46PA-75">[75]</a></sup> amusing others with "the cargoes of apparent rubbish which he frequently brought on board".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Much of the second day was taken up with excavating a large skull which Darwin found embedded in soft rock, and seemed to him to be allied to the <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">rhinoceros</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> On 8 October he returned to the site, and found a jawbone and tooth which he was able to identify using <a href="/wiki/Jean_Baptiste_Bory_de_Saint-Vincent" title="Jean Baptiste Bory de Saint-Vincent">Bory de Saint-Vincent</a>'s <i>Dictionnaire classique</i>. He wrote home describing this and the large skull as <i><a href="/wiki/Megatherium" title="Megatherium">Megatherium</a></i> fossils, or perhaps <i><a href="/wiki/Megalonyx" title="Megalonyx">Megalonyx</a></i>, and excitedly noted that the only specimens in Europe were locked away in the King's collection at <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k109_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-k109-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter188_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter188-79">[79]</a></sup> In the same layer he found a large surface of polygonal plates of bony armour. His immediate thought was that they came from an enormous armadillo like the small creatures common in the area, but from <a href="/wiki/Georges_Cuvier" title="Georges Cuvier">Cuvier</a>'s misleading description of the Madrid specimen and a recent newspaper report about a fossil found by <a href="/wiki/Woodbine_Parish" title="Woodbine Parish">Woodbine Parish</a>, Darwin thought that the bony armour identified the fossil as the <i>Megatherium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter192_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter192-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> With FitzRoy, Darwin went about 30 miles (48 km) across the bay to <a href="/wiki/Monte_Hermoso" title="Monte Hermoso">Monte Hermoso</a> on 19 October, and found numerous fossils of smaller <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> in contrast to the huge <a href="/wiki/Xenarthra" title="Xenarthra">Edentatal</a> mammals of Punta Alta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D46MH_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-D46MH-83">[83]</a></sup> In November at <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a> he "purchased fragments of some enormous bones" which he "was assured belonged to the former giants!!",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B64_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-B64-84">[84]</a></sup> and subsequently took any chance to get fossils "by gold or galloping".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B92_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-B92-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>At Montevideo in November the mail from home included a copy of the second volume of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lyell" title="Charles Lyell">Charles Lyell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Geology" title="Principles of Geology">Principles of Geology</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k01p27_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-k01p27-41">[41]</a></sup> a refutation of <a href="/wiki/Lamarckism" title="Lamarckism">Lamarckism</a> in which there was no shared ancestry of different species or overall progress to match the gradual geological change, but a continuing cycle in which species mysteriously appeared, closely adapted to their "centres of creation", then became extinct when the environment changed to their disadvantage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tierra_del_Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tierra del Fuego">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fuegian_BeagleVoyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="400" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Fuegian_BeagleVoyage.jpg/220px-Fuegian_BeagleVoyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Fuegian_BeagleVoyage.jpg/330px-Fuegian_BeagleVoyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/Fuegian_BeagleVoyage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fuegian_BeagleVoyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
His encounter with the natives of the <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> on his <i>Beagle</i> voyage made Darwin believe that <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> had evolved over time from a more primitive state.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>They reached <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> on 18 December 1832 and Darwin was taken aback at what he perceived as the crude savagery of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaghan" title="Yaghan">Yaghan</a> natives, in stark contrast to the "civilised" behaviour of the three <a href="/wiki/Fuegians" title="Fuegians">Fuegians</a> they were returning as missionaries (who had been given the names York Minster, Fuegia Basket and <a href="/wiki/Jemmy_Button" title="Jemmy Button">Jemmy Button</a>). He described his first meeting with the native Fuegians as being "without exception the most curious and interesting spectacle I ever beheld: I could not have believed how wide was the difference between savage and civilised man: it is greater than between a wild and domesticated animal, inasmuch as in man there is a greater power of improvement." In contrast, he said of Jemmy that "It seems yet wonderful to me, when I think over all his many good qualities, that he should have been of the same race, and doubtless partaken of the same character, with the miserable, degraded savages whom we first met here." (Four decades later, he recalled these impressions in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Descent_of_Man" title="The Descent of Man">The Descent of Man</a></i> to support his argument that just as humans had descended from "a lower form", civilised society had arisen by graduations from a more primitive state. He recalled how closely the Fuegians on board the <i>Beagle</i> "resembled us in disposition and in most of our mental faculties."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>)</p>
<p>At the island of "Buttons Land" on 23 January 1833 they set up a mission post, with huts, gardens, furniture and crockery, but when they returned nine days later the possessions had been looted and divided up equally by the natives. Matthews gave up, rejoining the ship and leaving the three civilised Fuegians to continue the missionary work. The <i>Beagle</i> went on to the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a> arriving just after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Re-establishment_of_British_rule_on_the_Falklands_(1833)" title="Re-establishment of British rule on the Falklands (1833)">British return</a>. Darwin studied the relationships of species to habitats and found ancient fossils like those he had found in Wales. Fitzroy bought a <a href="/wiki/Schooner" title="Schooner">schooner</a> to assist with the surveying, and they returned to Patagonia where this was fitted with a new copper bottom and renamed <i>Adventure</i>. Darwin was assisted by <a href="/wiki/Syms_Covington" title="Syms Covington">Syms Covington</a> in preserving specimens and his collecting was so successful that with FitzRoy's agreement he took on Covington as a full-time servant for £30 a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gauchos.2C_rheas.2C_fossils_and_geology">Gauchos, rheas, fossils and geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Second_voyage_of_HMS_Beagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Gauchos, rheas, fossils and geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The two ships sailed to the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Negro_(Argentina)" title="Río Negro (Argentina)">Río Negro</a> in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> and on 8 August 1833 Darwin left on another journey inland with the <a href="/wiki/Gaucho" title="Gaucho">gauchos</a>. On 12 August he met General <a href="/wiki/Juan_Manuel_de_Rosas" title="Juan Manuel de Rosas">Juan Manuel de Rosas</a> who was then leading a <a href="/wiki/Punitive_expedition" title="Punitive expedition">punitive expedition</a> in <a href="/wiki/Desert_Campaign_(1833%E2%80%9334)" title="Desert Campaign (1833–34)">his military campaign</a> against <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Argentina" title="Indigenous peoples in Argentina">native "Indians"</a>, and obtained a passport from him. As they crossed the <a href="/wiki/Pampas" title="Pampas">pampas</a> the gauchos and Indians told Darwin of a rare smaller species of <a href="/wiki/Darwin%27s_rhea" title="Darwin's rhea">rhea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B63-271_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-B63-271-89">[89]</a></sup> After three days at <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%ADa_Blanca" title="Bahía Blanca">Bahía Blanca</a> he grew tired of waiting for the <i>Beagle</i> and on 21 August revisited Punta Alta where he reviewed the geology of the site in light of his new knowledge, wondering if the bones were older than the seashells. He was very successful with searching for bones, and on 1 September found a near complete skeleton with its bones still in position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter215_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter215-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>He set off again and on 1 October searching the cliffs of the <a href="/wiki/Carcara%C3%B1%C3%A1_River" title="Carcarañá River">Carcarañá River</a> found "an enormous gnawing tooth" then in a cliff of the <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_River" title="Paraná River">Paraná River</a> saw "two great groups of immense bones" which were too soft to collect but a tooth fragment identified them as <a href="/wiki/Mastodon" title="Mastodon">mastodons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter229_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter229-93">[93]</a></sup> Illness delayed him at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_Argentina" title="Santa Fe, Argentina">Santa Fe</a>, and after seeing the fossilised casing of a huge <a href="/wiki/Armadillo" title="Armadillo">armadillo</a> embedded in rock, he was puzzled to find a horse tooth in the same rock layer, since horses had been introduced to the continent with European migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StFeintro_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-StFeintro-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> They took a riverboat down the Paraná River to <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a> but became entangled in <a href="/wiki/Revolution_of_the_Restorers" title="Revolution of the Restorers">a revolution</a> as rebels allied to Rosas blockaded the city. The passport helped and with Covington he managed to escape in a boatload of refugees. They rejoined the <i>Beagle</i> at <a href="/wiki/Montevideo" title="Montevideo">Montevideo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>As surveys were still in progress Darwin set off on another 400 mile (600 km) "galloping" trip in <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Banda Oriental</a> to see the <a href="/wiki/Uruguay_River" title="Uruguay River">Uruguay River</a> and visit the Estancia of Mr Keen near <a href="/wiki/Mercedes,_Uruguay" title="Mercedes, Uruguay">Mercedes</a> on the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Negro_(Uruguay)" title="Río Negro (Uruguay)">Río Negro</a>. On 25 November he "heard of some giants bones, which as usual turned out to be those of the Megatherium" but could only extract a few broken fragments, then on the next day visited a nearby house and bought for about two <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shillings</a> "a head of a Megatherium which must have been when found quite perfect", though the teeth had since been broken and the lower jaw had been lost. Mr Keen arranged to ship the skull down river to Buenos Aires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter238_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letter238-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-introBandaOriental_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-introBandaOriental-99">[99]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Las_Piedras,_Uruguay" title="Las Piedras, Uruguay">Las Piedras</a> a clergyman let him see fossils including a club-like tail which he sketched and called an "extraordinary weapon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-introBandaOriental_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-introBandaOriental-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BO36_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-BO36-100">[100]</a></sup> His notes included a page showing his realisation that the cliff banks of the rivers exposed two strata formed in an <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a> interrupted by an undersea stratum, indicating that the land had risen and fallen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BO37_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-BO37-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Image-Rhea_Darwinii1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="601" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Image-Rhea_Darwinii1.jpg/220px-Image-Rhea_Darwinii1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Image-Rhea_Darwinii1.jpg/330px-Image-Rhea_Darwinii1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Image-Rhea_Darwinii1.jpg/440px-Image-Rhea_Darwinii1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Image-Rhea_Darwinii1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of <a href="/wiki/Darwin%27s_rhea" title="Darwin's rhea">Darwin's rhea</a>, published in 1841 in <a href="/wiki/John_Gould" title="John Gould">John Gould</a>'s description of birds collected on the <i>Beagle</i> voyage.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Back at Montevideo, Darwin was introduced to <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Martens" title="Conrad Martens">Conrad Martens</a>, the replacement artist brought on board the <i>Beagle</i> after Augustus Earle had to leave due to health problems. They sailed south, putting in at <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Deseado" title="Puerto Deseado">Port Desire</a> on 23 December, and the following day Darwin shot a <a href="/wiki/Guanaco" title="Guanaco">guanaco</a> which provided them with a Christmas meal. Early in the new year, Martens shot a rhea which they enjoyed eating before Darwin realised that this was the elusive smaller rhea, and preserved the remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B63-271_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-B63-271-89">[89]</a></sup> On 9 January 1834, 110 miles (180 km) further south, they reached <a href="/wiki/Puerto_San_Juli%C3%A1n" title="Puerto San Julián">Port St Julian</a> and exploring the <a href="/wiki/Patagonia#Geology" title="Patagonia">local geology</a> in cliffs near the harbour Darwin found fossils of pieces of spine and a hind leg of "some large animal, I fancy a Mastodon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letter238_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Letter238-98">[98]</a></sup> On 26 January they entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straits_of_Magellan" title="Straits of Magellan">Straits of Magellan</a> and at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=St._Gregory%27s_Bay&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="St. Gregory's Bay (page does not exist)">St. Gregory's Bay</a> they met half-civilised Patagonian "giants" over 6 ft (1.8 m) tall, described by Darwin as "excellent practical naturalists". One told him that the smaller rheas were the only species this far south, while the larger rheas kept to the north, the species meeting around the Rio Negro.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>After further surveying in Tierra del Fuego they returned on 5 March 1834 to visit the missionaries, but found the huts deserted.
